Divorce Tied to a Place: Immediate Effect Everywhere
Ruling that divorce tied to a place takes effect immediately everywhere

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband attaches divorce to a place and explain the diyanatan/qada'an distinction for hidden intentions.

Core Idea
Pronouncing divorce with a place-link takes effect immediately everywhere; a hidden condition is only accepted diyanatan, not in court.

You might think that saying 'you are divorced in Makkah' delays the divorce until Makkah. But Hanafi law says no—divorce is a verbal act, not tied to location. This lesson unpacks that ruling and explains when a husband's secret intention matters between him and Allah but not in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband says 'You are divorced in Makkah' (or 'by Makkah' or 'in the house'). The Hanafi ruling is that the divorce takes effect immediately in all places.
The specific phrases are 'bi Makkah', 'fi Makkah', and 'fi al-dar'.
2
Understand the rationale (dalil)
Divorce is a verbal pronouncement; it is not inherently tied to a location. The ruling is based on the principle that talaq cannot be restricted to a specific place (الطلاق لا يتخصص بمكان).
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli): the nature of divorce as an utterance precludes spatial limitation.
3
Apply the apparent meaning in court
In court (qada'an), the external wording is taken at face value. Since the apparent wording includes a place but does not make the divorce conditional on entering that place, the judge rules that divorce occurs immediately everywhere.
The court does not investigate hidden intentions unless there is evidence.
4
Distinguish hidden intention (diyanatan)
If the husband admits he meant 'when you come to Makkah,' that hidden intention is accepted only religiously (diyanatan) before Allah, not legally. The court enforces the apparent meaning.
The husband may be truthful before Allah but the wife is still legally divorced from the moment of utterance.
5
Note the practical consequence
The thamarah: the wife is immediately divorced and can remarry after iddah; the husband's secret condition does not affect the legal status.
This ruling is consistent across all three phrases mentioned (bi Makkah, fi Makkah, fi al-dar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال انتِ طالق بمكة اوفى مكة فهي طالق فى الحال فى كل البلاد وكذلك لو قال انت طالق في الدارلان الطلاق لا يتخصص بمكان دون مكان وان عنى به اذا اتيت مكة يصدق ديانة لاقضاء لانه نوى الاضمار وهو خلاف الظاهر ترجمہ اور اگر کہا تو طلاق والی ہے مکہ کے ساتھ یا مکہ میں تو یہ فی الحال تمام شہروں میں طلاق والی ہوگی ۔ اور ایسے ہی اگر کہا انت طالق في الدار کیونکہ طلاق ایسی نہیں کہ ایک مکان کے ساتھ خاص ہو نہ کہ دوسرے کے۔ اور اگر مراد لیا اس سے اذا اتيت مكة تو دیات تصدیق کی جائے گی نہ کہ قضاء ۔ کیونکہ اس شخص نے مخفی بات کی نیت کی ہے اور وہ خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ ایک مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت طالق بمكة يا فى مكة يا انت طالق في الدار - تو طلاق فی الحال واقع ہو گی۔ اور یہ عورت دنیا بھر کے تمام شہروں اور روئے زمین میں مطلقہ کہلائے گی ۔ کیونکہ طلاق کسی ایک مکان کیساتھ خاص ہو نہ کہ دوسرے کے ایسا نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ جب واقع ہوگی تو ہر جگہ پر واقع ہوگی ۔ اور اگر اس مرد نے بمكة او في مكة سے اذا اتيت مکہ کا ارادہ کیا یعنی وقوع طلاق کیلئے ایان مک کا اردو بھی چیز کا ارادہ ہے جو خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ اور ماقبل میں گذر چکا کہ خلاف ظاہر کی دیانا تصدیق کی جاتی ہے قضاء نہیں ۔
And if he said, 'You are divorced by Makkah' or 'in Makkah,' then she is divorced immediately in all cities. Likewise if he said, 'You are divorced in the house,' because divorce is not specific to one place to the exclusion of another. And if he intended by it 'when you come to Makkah,' he is believed in religious terms (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an), because he intended a hidden meaning contrary to the apparent. Translation: And if he said, 'You are divorced by Makkah' or 'in Makkah,' then she is divorced immediately in all cities. Similarly if he said 'You are divorced in the house.' Explanation: A man said to his wife 'You are divorced by Makkah' or 'in Makkah' or 'You are divorced in the house' – the divorce occurs immediately. Rather, when it occurs, it occurs everywhere. Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage explains that if a husband links divorce to a specific place, such as 'You are divorced in Makkah' or 'in the house,' the divorce takes effect immediately, not when she goes there. This is because talaq is not confined to locations; it is a verbal pronouncement that, once uttered, takes effect immediately regardless of place. If the husband claims he meant that the divorce would occur only when she goes to that place (i.e., a conditional divorce), his hidden intention is not accepted in court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent wording. However, he is believed in religious matters (diyanatan) between him and Allah. The ruling applies uniformly to 'by Makkah,' 'in Makkah,' and 'in the house.'

Key Terms
ديانة (diyanatan): Religiously; in terms of one's private conscience before Allah. A hidden intention is accepted diyanatan but not in court (qada'an).
قضاء (qada'an): In court; legally enforceable. A statement contrary to apparent meaning is judged by the external wording in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A divorce tied to a place takes effect immediately everywhere; a hidden condition is only accepted diyanatan, not in court.

Conditional Divorce on Entering Mecca: When It Occurs
Ruling on conditional divorce using 'when' vs 'upon entering'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional and temporal expressions in divorce and apply the Hanafi rule that phrases like 'fi dukhulik' are treated as conditions.

Core Idea
When a container cannot contain divorce, it becomes a condition.

In Hanafi divorce law, precise wording determines the timing of divorce. This passage analyzes two similar phrases: 'idha dakhalti Makkah' (when you enter Mecca) and 'fi dukhulik al-dar' (upon your entering the house). The key insight is that both are interpreted as conditions suspending divorce until the event occurs, based on linguistic principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Note the difference: 'idha dakhalti Makkah' uses the clear conditional particle 'idha', while 'fi dukhulik al-dar' uses the preposition 'fi'.
2
Recognize the ruling for 'idha'
The text states that 'idha' clearly suspends divorce until entry; it is a straightforward condition.
3
Analyze 'fi dukhulik'
'Fi' is normally a container (zarf), but divorce cannot be contained in an action. Therefore, it is reinterpreted as a condition (shart) due to its closeness in meaning.
The parallelism between zarf and shart allows this interpretation.
4
Apply the same ruling
Both phrases result in the same ruling: divorce occurs only when the wife enters the specified place.
5
Consider the proof (talil)
The proof is linguistic: when a zarf cannot be literal, it is treated as a shart to preserve meaning. This is a form of reasoning by necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال انت طالق اذا دخلت مكة لم تطلق حتى تدخل مكة لانه علقه بالدخول ولوقال في دخولك الدار يتعلق بالفعل لمقارنة بين الشرط والظرف فحمل عليه عند تعذر الظرفية ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت طالق از ادخلت مکہ تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی یہاں تک کہ مکہ میں داخل ہو جائے۔ کیونکہ طلاق کو دخول ( مکہ ) پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور اگر کہانی دخولک الدار تو فعل کے ساتھ متعلق ہو گی۔ شرط اور ظرف کے درمیان مقارنت کی وجہ سے۔ پس (ظرف) کو شرط پر محمول کیا جائے گا ظرفیت مستعد رہونے کے وقت۔ تشریح اور اگر کہا انست طالق اذا دخلت مكة تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی یہاں تک کہ مکہ میں داخل ہو جائے ۔ کیونکہ طلاق کو دخول (مکہ) پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور اگر طلاق غد کے اول جز میں واقع ہو۔ اور اگر فی دخولک الدار کہا تو فعل کیساتھ متعلق ہوگی ظرف اور شرط کے ساتھ مقارنت کی وجہ سے پس ظرف کو شرط پر محمول کریں گے ظرفیت کے متبعد ر ہونے کے وقت ۔
And if he said, 'You are divorced when you enter Makkah,' she is not divorced until she enters Makkah, because he suspended the divorce on entry. And if he said, 'upon your entering the house,' it is related to the verb due to the closeness between the condition and the container, so it is interpreted as a condition when being a container is impossible. And if he says, 'upon your entering the house,' it is connected to the verb because of the parallelism between the condition and the container; thus, it is interpreted as a condition when being a container is not possible. Explanation: And if he said, 'You are divorced when you enter Makkah,' the divorce does not occur until she enters Makkah, because he suspended divorce on entry. And if the divorce occurs in the first part of tomorrow. And if he says 'upon your entering the house,' it is related to the verb because of the parallelism between condition and container; therefore, we interpret the container as a condition when being a container is not possible.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of conditional phrases: 'when you enter Makkah' (idha dakhalti Makkah) and 'upon your entering the house' (fi dukhulik al-dar). The first clearly acts as a condition (shart) for divorce; it does not happen until the condition is fulfilled. The second, 'fi dukhulik al-dar,' uses the word 'fi' (in/upon). The commentator explains that because 'fi' followed by a verbal noun (dukhul) resembles both a container (zarf) and a condition (shart), and since divorce cannot be a container for an action, it is interpreted as a condition (shart) to give meaning. Thus, 'upon your entering' is treated as a condition, so divorce occurs upon entry. The mention of 'first part of tomorrow' (ghad) in the commentary seems to be a comparison with temporal conditions, indicating that when a time is specified for divorce, it occurs at the beginning of that time unless otherwise intended.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): Condition; a future event upon which the occurrence of divorce depends.
ظرف (zarf): Container; in grammar, an adverb of time or place. Here, 'fi' is a zarf but in this context it is interpreted as a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says, 'You are divorced upon your entering the house' (fi dukhulik al-dar), when does the divorce occur?
A) Immediately
B) At the beginning of the next day
C) When she enters the house
D) Never, because it is meaningless

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, phrases like 'upon your entering' are interpreted as conditions for divorce, so the divorce only takes effect when the act is done.

Temporal Addition of Divorce: 'Tomorrow' at Dawn
Ruling on divorce with future time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce with a future time and apply the rule that the divorce occurs at the earliest part of the specified time.

Core Idea
Divorce conditional on time takes effect at dawn of that day, unless hidden intention proved religiously.

This passage introduces a key rule in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband attaches a future time to his pronouncement of divorce, the divorce takes effect at the earliest moment of that time. The default is the beginning of the day, dawn (fajr). However, the husband's secret contrary intention is accepted in religious conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an). This lesson examines the reasoning behind this default.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan). The ruling: divorce occurs at dawn of tomorrow.
2

Because the word 'tomorrow' covers the entire day, and the divorce is effective from the first part (dawn) by default, as the earliest possible moment.
3
Distinguish diyanatan vs qada'an
If the husband intended the end of the day, he is believed in religious matters (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) because it contradicts the apparent general meaning.

Ruling on Divorce Mentioning a Location: Immediate Effect Everywhere
Explains that specifying a location does not restrict divorce to that place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a location is mentioned in a divorce pronouncement and distinguish between the apparent meaning and hidden intention in terms of diyanatan and qadaan.

Core Idea
Mentioning a location in divorce does not restrict its effect; divorce takes effect immediately everywhere.

This passage addresses a common misunderstanding: when a husband says 'You are divorced in Mecca' or 'in the house', does the divorce only take effect in that location? The Hanafi ruling is clear: divorce is a legal status not tied to any place, so it occurs immediately and universally. Additionally, if the husband secretly intended a condition (e.g., 'when you enter Mecca'), that intention is not considered in court but is accepted in his private conscience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronouncement
Read the husband's words: 'You are divorced in Mecca' or 'in the house'. Note that the location is mentioned explicitly.
The apparent meaning suggests the divorce is linked to that place.
2
Determine the ruling on its effect
According to the text, divorce occurs immediately and the wife is divorced everywhere, regardless of the location mentioned.
The ruling is based on the nature of divorce as a universal legal status.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
Divorce is not specific to one place; once validly pronounced, it applies in all locations. The mention of a location does not create a condition.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the legal nature of talaq.
4
Distinguish the hidden intention scenario
If the husband intended 'when you enter Mecca' (a condition), that hidden intention contradicts the apparent meaning. In court (qadaan), the apparent meaning is taken, so divorce occurs immediately. But in private conscience (diyanatan), his intention is accepted.
This follows the Hanafi principle that hidden intentions contrary to the apparent are only considered between the person and Allah.

Similarly if he said 'You are divorced in the house', because divorce is not specific to one house and not another. And if he intended by this 'when you enter Mecca', then he will be believed dianatan (in private conscience) not qadaan (in court). However, in his private conscience (dianatan), he is believed, as per the principle that hidden intentions contrary to the apparent are accepted only in matters between him and Allah.

Key Terms

Divorce Suspended Upon a Condition: The Hanafi Ruling on 'Idha Dakhalti Makkah'
Ruling on conditional divorce with 'idha dakhalti Makkah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce using 'idha' and distinguish it from the phrase 'fi dukhulik al-dar', applying the principle of ta'liq.

Core Idea
Divorce is deferred until the condition occurs — ta'liq suspends the talaq, not the pronouncement.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi divorce law: the suspension (ta'liq) of a divorce upon a future condition. The passage clarifies that when a husband says 'You are divorced when you enter Mecca,' the divorce does not take effect until the condition of entry is actually fulfilled. It also contrasts this with a similar expression using 'upon your entry' to illustrate how the language determines the legal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the suspended divorce phrase
The husband says 'You are divorced when you enter Mecca.' The word 'idha' introduces a future condition. Note that the divorce is pronounced but its effect is suspended.
The phrase is a ta'liq (suspension), not an immediate pronouncement.
2
Apply the ta'liq rule
According to Hanafi jurisprudence, a divorce conditional upon a future event is valid but does not take effect until the condition is fulfilled. Here, until she actually enters Mecca.
The text says 'lam tutallaq hatta tadkhula Makkah.'
3
Distinguish the similar phrase 'upon your entry'
If the husband says 'upon your entry into the house' (fi dukhulik al-dar), the preposition 'fi' is originally adverbial. However, because an action (entry) cannot be a place, the phrase is treated as a condition (shart) rather than a time/place adverbial. The ruling is the same: divorce occurs only upon entry.
The comparability (mugarinah) between condition and adverbial leads to carrying the adverbial upon the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انت طالق اذا دخلت مکۃ سے کب طلاق واقع ہوگی
وَلَوْ قَالَ انت طالق اذا دخلت مكة لم تطلق حتى تدخل مكة لانه علقه بالدخول ولوقال في دخولك الدار يتعلق بالفعل لمقارنة بين الشرط والظرف فحمل عليه عند تعذر الظرفية

And if he said 'upon your entry into the house', it is attached to the action due to the comparability between the condition and the adverbial phrase, so it is carried upon it when adverbiality is not possible. Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a conditional divorce using the word 'idha' (when). If a man says 'You are divorced when you enter Mecca', the divorce does not occur until the condition of entry is actually fulfilled. This is because the divorce is suspended (mu'allaq) upon a future event. The text then distinguishes a similar phrase: if he says 'upon your entry into the house' (fi dukhulik al-dar), the word 'fi' is an adverbial particle, but here it is treated as a condition (shart) because the adverbial meaning is not possible (entry is an action, not a place). Therefore, the ruling is the same: divorce is conditional upon the action of entry. The commentary reiterates the main point and adds a comparison with divorce at the first part of the next day (which is a separate issue). The core legal point is that conditional divorce ('alaqah) is valid and divorce is deferred until the condition occurs.

Key Terms
تعلیق (ta‘līq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon a future condition; the divorce is not effective until the condition is fulfilled.

Divorce Effective at Dawn of the Next Day
Ruling on divorce attributed to a future day (ghadan).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default time of divorce when a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' and explain the difference between dianatan and qadaan rulings in such a case.

Core Idea
Divorce attributed to 'tomorrow' takes effect at dawn unless the husband privately intended a later time.

When a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (anti taliq ghadan), the divorce is linked to the future day. This lesson examines when exactly that divorce occurs in Hanafi law. The default rule is that it occurs at the very beginning of the day, i.e., fajr, but the husband’s private intention for a later moment may be accepted in his conscience, though not in court. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying rulings on conditional and time-attributed divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and the word 'ghadan'
The key utterance is 'anti taliq ghadan' (you are divorced tomorrow). The word 'ghadan' means the next day, which in Arabic usage refers to the entire day from fajr to sunset.
2
Apply the default rule: divorce occurs at fajr
Because 'tomorrow' is a general term covering the whole day, the divorce is considered to occur in the first part of that day, i.e., at the break of dawn (fajr). This is the apparent (zahir) meaning.
The text compares this to describing her as divorced in 'all of tomorrow'.
3
Examine the exception: intention for a specific part of the day
If the husband intended the divorce to take effect at the end of the day (e.g., sunset) rather than the beginning, his intention is accepted privately (dianatan) but not in court (qadaan).
Dianatan means he is believed in his own conscience; qadaan means the court will go by the apparent meaning.
4
Understand the rationale for the court’s position
The court does not accept a claim that contradicts the apparent meaning because people in divorce matters often speak loosely and may seek a benefit, such as delaying the divorce.
5
Distinguish between dianatan and qadaan
Dianatan: the husband's word is accepted regarding his own religious duty; qadaan: the court follows the default ruling (fajr) and does not recognize a hidden intention.
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: intention can affect the ruling before Allah but not necessarily in a judicial verdict.

Source Text

فصل في اضافة الطلاق الى الزمان

آفتاب تک کے تمام اجزاء و لمحات میر ستحقق ( ثابت ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ ایام بیض ، ایام تشریق، ایام صیام سے واضح ہے۔ سورۃ القدر میں مطلع الفجر سے ” دن“ کا ابتدائی جزو اور محی اور اتموا الصيام الی اللیل سے دن کا آخری جزو اور لمحہ مراد ہے۔ جاننا چاہئے کہ لفظ اس۔ گذشتہ کل کیلئے اور لفظ غداً آئندہ کل کیلئے مستعمل ہوتا ہے۔ لفظ غدا قرآن مجید میں بھی مذکورہ ہے۔ مثال کے طور پر نفس ما قدمت لغد اور ماذا تكسب غداً چنانچہ ہر دو آیات میں آئندہ والی کل مذکور ہے۔ یعنی اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی بیوی سے یوں کہا کہ آئندہ آنے والی کل ( غدا) میں تجھ پر طلاق واقع ہو تو جس وقت آنے والی کل متحقق (ثابت) ہوگی اس غدا " کا تحقق ( ثبوت) طلوع فجر کے پہلے جزو اور لمحے سے ہوگا یعنی جو نہی اگلے دن کی طلوع فجر وجود میں آئے گی جو نہی غدا( آئندہ کل مستحق (ثابت) ہو گا لہذا طلوع فجر ہوتے ہی متکام کا کلام (انست طالق غداً ) اس کی بیوی پر مستحق (ثابت) ہو کر طلاق کو واقع کر دے گا۔ کیونکہ ”خدا“ کا لفظ آئندہ کل کیلئے موصوف ہے یتکلم کی بیوی پر طلاق کا وقوع بھی اسی وصف کے ساتھ متصف ہے اگر متکلم نے اپنی بیوی کے مطلقہ ہونے کو دن کے کسی مخصوص جز دولحہ کے ساتھ متصف کرنے کی نیت کی تو درمیں صورت دیا نیا متکلم کے کلام کا اعتبار کیا جائے گا لیکن قاضی کے ہاں دن کے مخصوص لمحے پر بنی کلام معتبر نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس ( متکلم ) نے دن کی عمومیت کو مخصوص کر دیا۔ درمیں صورت ظاہر کے خلاف کا احتمال مراد ہے بائیں وجہ کہ کل کے دن کسی وقت بھی متکلم کی بیوئی مطلقہ ہو سکتی ہے لہذا یاتنا متکلم کے کلام (انست طالق غدا ) کی تصدیق ہوگی لیکن قاضی اس کلام کی تصدیق نہیں کرے گا کیونکہ انسان عموماً طلاق کے معاملے میں بیہودہ کلام اختیار کرتا ہے اور اس میں اپنے فائدے کی بات تلاش کرتا ہے۔ لہذا یہ ظاہر کے خلاف ہوا۔
تشریح از محمد ابو بکر قاسمی ولد مولانا محمد عبد القادر قائمی)
Chapter on Adding Divorce to Time. Translation: Chapter on adding divorce to time. When does divorce occur from 'You are divorced tomorrow'? And if he said 'You are divorced tomorrow', divorce occurs upon her at the break of dawn, because he described her with divorce in all of the tomorrow, and that is tantamount to its occurrence in the first part of it. And if he intended by it the end of the day, he is believed dianatan, not qadaan, because he intended specification in the general, and it is possible, and it was contrary to the apparent. Translation: And if he said 'You are divorced tomorrow', then at the break of dawn of tomorrow she will be divorced. Because he described her as being divorced in relation to the entire day of tomorrow, which is tantamount to its occurrence in the first part of it. And if he intended by it the end of the day (the last part), he will be believed dianatan, not qadaan, because he intended specification in the general (word 'tomorrow'), and it is possible, and it was contrary to the apparent. Explanation: In every language of the world, different words are used for 'day'. So among the languages of the world, one is Arabic, which is broader than all other languages. In Arabic, for 'day' the words 'yawm' and 'nahar' are used. For example, Yawm al-Qiyamah (Day of Resurrection), Yawm al-'Eid (Day of Eid). The plural of 'yawm' is 'ayyam'. This word (yawm) appears in many verses of the Noble Quran. When the word 'day' comes on the tongue, the mind conceives all the parts of the day from beginning to end. From this it is understood that 'day' does not mean a specific moment or time (hour, minute, second etc.). The application of 'day' (yawm, nahar) is realized from the break of dawn to sunset of all parts and moments, as is clear from al-ayyam al-bid, ayyam al-tashriq, ayyam al-siyam. In Surah al-Qadr, 'the break of dawn' refers to the initial part of the day, and in 'then complete the fast until the night' refers to the last part and moment of the day. It should be known that the word 'ams' is used for yesterday, and the word 'ghadan' is used for tomorrow. The word 'ghadan' is also mentioned in the Noble Quran. For example, 'nafs ma qaddamat li-ghad' and 'mada taksubu ghadan'. So in both verses, the upcoming tomorrow is mentioned. That is, if a person said to his wife, 'Tomorrow (ghadan) you are divorced', then at the time the upcoming tomorrow is realized, its realization (thubut) is from the first part and moment of the break of dawn. So the break of dawn of the next day comes into existence, and thus 'ghadan' (upcoming tomorrow) is realized (thabut). Therefore, at the break of dawn, the speaker's speech (anti taliq ghadan) is realized upon his wife and causes divorce to occur. Because the word 'ghadan' is described for the upcoming tomorrow, and the occurrence of divorce upon the speaker's wife is also described with that same description. If the speaker intended to describe his wife's being divorced with a specific part or moment of the day, then in that case, dianatan the speaker's word will be considered, but in the qadi's court, the word based on a specific moment of the day will not be considered, because he (the speaker) specified the generality of the day. In that case, the contrary to the apparent is intended, because the wife of the speaker can be divorced at any time of the day tomorrow. Therefore, the speaker's word (anti taliq ghadan) will be believed, but the qadi will not believe this speech because people generally adopt frivolous speech in matters of divorce and seek something beneficial to themselves. Therefore, it is contrary to the apparent. Explanation by Muhammad Abu Bakr Qasimi, son of Maulana Muhammad Abdul Qadir Qasimi).
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and ruling about adding divorce to a time (idafat al-talaq ila al-zaman). The specific ruling is that if a man says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (anti taliq ghadan), the divorce takes effect at the break of dawn (fajr) of the next day. The reason is that the word 'ghadan' (tomorrow) refers to the entire future day, and the divorce is considered to occur at the first part of that day by default. However, if the man intended the divorce to take effect at the end of the day (i.e., the last moment), his intention is accepted only in his private conscience (dianatan), not in court. The commentary extensively explains the Arabic word 'yawm' (day) and 'ghadan' (tomorrow), citing Quranic verses, to establish that 'day' encompasses from fajr to sunset. The author then reiterates the legal ruling and emphasizes that the court follows the apparent meaning (zahir), and a claim of intending a specific part of the day is contrary to the apparent and thus not accepted judicially.

Key Terms
غداً (ghadan): Tomorrow, the next day, starting from fajr.
اضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان (iḍāfat al-ṭalāq ilā al-zamān): Adding or attributing the divorce to a specific time, such as saying 'You are divorced tomorrow'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'You are divorced tomorrow (ghadan)' at 11 PM. When does the divorce take effect according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) Immediately at 11 PM.
B) At the next midnight.
C) At the break of dawn (fajr) of the next day.
D) At sunset of the next day.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow', the divorce defaults to the start of that day (fajr) in court, but his private intention for a later time is accepted religiously.

When Conflicting Time Words Are Used in Divorce
Ruling when contradictory time words are used in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a man uses contradictory time words in divorce and explain the legal reasoning based on tanjiz and idafa.

Core Idea
In contradictory time words in divorce, the first word governs; the second is void.

When a husband utters a divorce with two conflicting time references, Hanafi law resolves the ambiguity by prioritizing the first word uttered. This ruling is rooted in the principles of tanjiz (immediacy) and idafa (deferment). Understanding this case sharpens your ability to identify which part of a pronouncement is operative and which is لغو (void).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible sequences
The husband may say 'today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today'. Recognize the two scenarios.
2
Determine the first word
The first word uttered is the key. Note which word comes first in the husband's utterance.
3
Apply the ruling
If the first word is 'today', the divorce is immediate (tanjiz). If it is 'tomorrow', the divorce is deferred to the next day (idafa).
4
Understand the reasons
The first word creates either tanjiz or idafa. The second word is void because it would contradict the first; an immediate cannot be combined with an addition, and vice versa.
5
Note the scope
The second word is لغو (void) in both cases. The divorce is valid based on the first word alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انت طالق اليوم غداً يا غداً اليوم کہنے سے طلاق کا حکم
وَلَوْ قَالَ انت طالق اليوم غدا او غداليوم فانه يؤخذ باول الوقتين الذى تفوه به فيقع في الأول في اليوم وفى الثاني في الغدلانه لما قال اليوم كان تنجيزاً والمنجز لا يحتمل الاضافة ولوقال غدا كان اضافة والمضاف لا يتنجز لما فيه من ابطال الاضافة فلغا اللفظ الثاني في الفصلين
ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت طالق اليوم غدا یا کہا انت طالق غدا الليوم توان دو وقتوں میں سے اسکو لیا جائے گا۔ جس کیساتھ
Ruling of divorce from saying 'You are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today'. And if he said 'You are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today', then the first of the two times that he uttered is taken, so it occurs in the first, if it was 'today' it occurs in 'today', and if it was 'tomorrow' it occurs in 'tomorrow'. Because when he said 'today', it was immediate (tanjiz) and the immediate does not admit of addition (idafa). And if he said 'tomorrow', it was addition, and the added (mudaf) is not immediate because it would nullify the addition. So the second word is void in both cases. Translation: And if he said ... taken from the two times, the one with which...
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage deals with a situation where a man uses two conflicting time words in a single divorce pronouncement, such as 'You are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today'. The ruling is that only the first time word is taken into effect. If he first says 'today', the divorce is immediate (tanjiz) and the word 'tomorrow' is ignored because it would contradict the immediacy. If he first says 'tomorrow', the divorce is deferred to the next day (addition), and the word 'today' is void because it would negate the addition. In both cases, the second word is لغو (void). The principle is that an immediate pronouncement (munajjaz) cannot be combined with an addition (idafa), and an addition cannot be made immediate. Therefore, the effective time is determined by the first word uttered.

Key Terms
تنجیز (tanjīz): Immediate, unconditional effect; the divorce takes effect at the moment of utterance without any delay.
اضافة (iḍāfa): Attribution or addition to a future time; the divorce is deferred to a specified future time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says 'You are divorced tomorrow today' (ghadan al-yawma), when is the divorce effective?
A) Immediately (today).
B) Tomorrow (ghadan).
C) Both times, but divided.
D) The divorce is invalid due to contradiction.

Key Takeaway
When contradictory time words are used in talaq, the first word uttered determines the timing, and the second word is void due to the incompatibility of tanjiz and idafa.

Ruling on Intention in Divorce Conditioned on Tomorrow (Ghadan)
Defines 'day' and 'tomorrow', ruling on diyanah vs qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a divorce occurs if a man says 'anti taliq ghadan' and explain the distinction between diyanah and qada in accepting his intention.

Core Idea
A divorce conditioned on 'tomorrow' occurs at Fajr; intention for a later part is accepted only between man and Allah, not in court.

This lesson examines a key rule in Hanafi talaq: when a man says 'you are divorced tomorrow', the divorce takes effect at the first moment of the next day (Fajr). But what if he intended a specific later part? The Hanafi school distinguishes between what is accepted in religious conscience (diyanah) and what is enforceable in court (qada). This passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies this distinction using Quranic definitions of 'day'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its default ruling
Locate the phrase 'anti taliq ghadan' and note that the divorce occurs at the first part of the coming day (Fajr). This is the literal consequence because 'ghadan' encompasses the whole day.
The first part of the day in Islamic law is Fajr.
2
Define 'ghadan' and 'yawm' as legal terms
Extract the definitions from the text: 'yawm' is from Fajr to sunset, and 'ghadan' means the following day starting from Fajr. Understand that these are not 24-hour periods but daylight periods.
Quranic verses (Surah al-Qadr 97:5, Surah al-Baqarah 2:187) are cited to establish these boundaries.
3
Analyze the diyanah vs qada distinction
The man may claim he intended a later part of the day (e.g., the last hour). In matters of religious conscience (diyanatan), he is believed, but in court (qada'), the intention is disregarded. Determine why: because he contradicted the apparent meaning (zahir) by specifying a part, and courts rely on apparent meanings to prevent manipulation.
The text notes that people often speak frivolously in divorce matters.
4
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical scenario
Given a specific claim (e.g., 'I intended the last moment of the day'), decide the outcome: legally, the divorce occurred at Fajr; religiously, the man must act according to his intention.
The judge does not accept the contrary intention because it opposes the apparent meaning.

It is described as being related to the entire day. The occurrence of this (divorce) will be equivalent to its occurrence in the first part of the day. And if the man intended the last part of the day from this (statement), then in terms of religion (diyanatan) it will be accepted, not in court (qada'), because he intended specification in the general, while it is probable, and he opposed the apparent meaning. Explanation: In every language of the world, different words are used for 'day'. Among the languages of the world, one is Arabic, which has vastness compared to all others. In Arabic, 'yawm' and 'nahar' are used for day. For example, Yawm al-Qiyamah (Day of Resurrection), Yawm al-Eid (Day of Eid). The plural of yawm is ayyam. This word is mentioned in many Quranic verses. When the word 'day' comes to the tongue, the concept of all parts from beginning to end arises in the mind. This shows that 'day' does not mean a specific moment or time. The application of day (yawm, nahar) is actualized from the dawn of fajr until sunset, as is clear from the days of Al-Ayyam al-Beed, Al-Ayyam al-Tashreeq, and the days of fasting. In Surah al-Qadr, 'the break of dawn' indicates the first part of the day, and in 'complete the fast until night' indicates the last part and moment of the day. Know that the word 'ams' is used for yesterday, and 'ghadan' for tomorrow. The word 'ghada' is also mentioned in the Quran. For example, 'nfs ma qaddamat li-ghad' and 'maza taksibu ghada' - both verses mention the coming day. So if a person says to his wife: 'Divorce will occur to you tomorrow (ghada)', then when the coming day is actualized, its actualization will be from the first part and moment of the dawn of fajr. That is, when the dawn of the next day comes into existence, the 'ghada' will be established. Therefore, upon the appearance of dawn, the speaker's statement becomes established on his wife and effects divorce. Because the word 'ghada' is attributed to the coming day, and the occurrence of divorce on the wife is also attributed with this attribute. If the speaker intended to characterize his wife's being divorced with a specific part or moment of the day, then in that case his statement will be considered in terms of religion (diyanatan), but with the judge, his statement based on the specific moment of the day will not be considered because he specified the generality of the day. In that case, what is meant is a probability contrary to the apparent meaning, because the wife could be divorced at any time of the day. Therefore, the speaker's statement will be accepted (in din), but the judge will not accept it, because people often adopt frivolous speech in divorce matters and seek their own benefit. So this is contrary to the apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage defines the legal understanding of 'day' (yawm) and 'tomorrow' (ghad) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins by stating a rule: if a man says 'anti taliq ghadan' (you are divorced tomorrow), the divorce occurs at the first part of the coming day, which is the dawn of fajr. However, if the man later claims that he intended a specific part of the day (e.g., the last part), his claim is accepted in matters of religious conscience (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'). The reason is that the word 'ghad' linguistically encompasses the entire coming day, so specifying a part is a departure from the apparent meaning. The court does not entertain such subjective intentions because people might manipulate words for personal advantage. The passage then explains the Quranic usage of 'yawm' and 'ghad' to support this linguistic definition. The author ultimately affirms that the divorce occurs at the first moment of the day, and any contrary intention is only effective between the man and Allah.

Key Terms
یوم (yawm): In this text, 'yawm' refers to the period from dawn of fajr until sunset, as used in Islamic law for divorce timing.
غدا (ghadan): Refers to the next day starting from fajr; in the context of divorce, it means the entire coming day.
دیانت (diyanatan): Acceptance of a claim in religious conscience (between the person and Allah), not enforceable by court.
قضاء (qada'): Legal judgment in court; a claim is accepted only if it conforms to apparent evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted both in din and in court.
B) It is accepted in din but not in court.
C) It is not accepted at all.
D) It is accepted only if he takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
A divorce conditioned on 'tomorrow' occurs at Fajr; any intention to specify a later part is only accepted between the husband and Allah, not in court.

Ruling on Contradictory Time Words in Divorce
Ruling on divorce when both 'today' and 'tomorrow' are mentioned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principles of tanjiz and idafa to determine the timing of divorce when contradictory time words are uttered.

Core Idea
When divorce is pronounced with two conflicting times, the first utterance governs; the second is void.

When a husband says 'You are divorced today, tomorrow,' he has used two time references. This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on which time takes effect and why. The key is understanding the concepts of tanjiz (immediate effect) and idafa (suspension to a future time).

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
Listen carefully to the divorce pronouncement and note both time words.
2
Determine the first time word
Identify which of the two time words was spoken first in the sentence.
3
Classify the first word
If the first word is 'al-yawma' (today), it indicates tanjiz (immediate). If it is 'ghadan' (tomorrow), it indicates idafa (postponement).
4
Apply the rule
If the first word is tanjiz, the second word is laghw (void) and does not affect the timing. If the first word is idafa, the second word is also laghw.
5
Determine the timing
The divorce takes effect at the time indicated by the first word: immediately if 'today', or the next day if 'tomorrow'.
The second word is always ignored.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولوقال انت طالق اليوم غدا او غداليوم فانه يؤخذ باول الوقتين الذى تفوه به فيقع في الأول في اليوم وفى الثاني في الغدلانه لما قال اليوم كان تنجيزاً والمنجز لا يحتمل الاضافة ولوقال غدا كان اضافة والمضاف لا يتنجز لما فيه من ابطال الاضافة فلغا اللفظ الثاني في الفصلين

Ruling on saying 'you are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today'. And if he said 'you are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today', then the first of the two times that he uttered will be taken. So in the first case it occurs in today, and in the second case it occurs in tomorrow. Because when he said 'today', it was an immediate divorce (tamjiz), and the immediate does not admit of addition (idafa). And if he said 'tomorrow', it is an addition, and something added does not become immediate because that would entail nullifying the addition. So the second word is nullified in both cases. Translation: And if he said 'anti taliq al-yawma ghadan' or 'anti taliq ghadan al-yawma', then the first time he uttered will be taken. So in the first case it occurs in today, and in the second case it occurs in tomorrow. Because when he said 'al-yawm', the divorce became immediate (mujazz), and the immediate does not admit of addition to tomorrow. And if he said 'ghada', then it becomes an addition, and something added cannot be immediate because that would require nullifying the addition. So in both cases, the second word is nullified. Explanation: If a man says to his wife 'anti taliq al-yawma ghadan' or 'anti taliq ghadan al-yawma', then the time he first uttered will be taken. Hence in the first case, the divorce occurs in tomorrow, and in the second case, in today. The proof is that when he said 'al-yawm', the divorce became immediate and does not admit of addition to tomorrow, so the word 'ghad' is nullified. And if he first said 'ghada', then the divorce is added to tomorrow, and something added cannot be immediate because that would nullify the addition, so the word 'al-yawm' is nullified.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a man uses contradictory time words in a single divorce pronouncement. The ruling is based on the linguistic principle that the first word takes effect. If he first says 'al-yawma' (today), the divorce is immediate (tamjiz) and cannot be postponed, so the subsequent 'ghadan' (tomorrow) is ignored. Conversely, if he first says 'ghadan', it is an added divorce (idafa), meaning it is postponed to the coming day, and the subsequent 'al-yawma' is ignored because it would nullify the addition. The text explains the reasoning: 'al-yawm' gives a present, unconditional divorce, while 'ghadan' creates a future condition. The second word is 'laghw' (void) because it contradicts the first. This is a straightforward application of Hanafi principles of ta'jiz and idafa in divorce.

Key Terms
منجر (munjiz): Immediate, unconditional divorce that takes effect instantly upon utterance.
اضافت (idafah): Addition or suspension of divorce to a future time or condition.
لغو (laghw): Void, null, without legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When contradictory time words are used in divorce, the first utterance determines the timing; the second is nullified.

The Effect of 'Fi' in Talaq: Intention to Specify the End of Day
Intra-Hanafi dispute on accepting intention to specify end of day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa accepts the husband's intention to specify the end of the day in 'anti taliq fi ghadin' and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument based on linguistic analogy.

Core Idea
'Fi' indicates a container, not full coverage; thus intention for a specific part overrides the default first part.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'anti taliq fi ghadin' (you are divorced in tomorrow). The central question is whether the husband's intention to specify the end of the day is accepted in court. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn differ based on their interpretation of the word 'fi' and its implications for time coverage. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to analyze linguistic arguments in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and default ruling
The husband says 'anti taliq fi ghadin'. Without any intention, the divorce occurs at the first part of tomorrow. Note that the word 'fi' is present.
The default first part is called 'ta'yeen daruri' (necessary specification).
2
Understand the dispute over intention
If the husband claims he intended the end of the day, does the court accept his intention? Abu Hanifa says yes; Sahibayn say no.
This is not about the validity of the divorce but the timing accepted in court.
3
Compare Abu Hanifa's linguistic argument
Abu Hanifa argues that 'fi' is a zarf (container) and does not imply isti'ab (full coverage). The default first part is only due to the absence of an opposing factor. Since the husband has a specific intention, his 'ta'yeen qasdi' (intended specification) takes precedence.
The key: 'fi' does not require coverage, so intention can override the default.
4
Compare Sahibayn's analogy
Sahibayn hold that 'fi ghadin' is equivalent to 'ghadan' (tomorrow), which implies full coverage of the day. They analogize to oaths: 'I will fast my whole life' covers all days, while 'I will fast in my life' does not. Thus, the husband cannot limit the divorce to a part.
For Sahibayn, 'fi' adds nothing because 'ghadan' itself is a zarf; the default first part remains.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, if the husband intended the end of the day, the divorce occurs at maghrib (end of day). Under Sahibayn, it occurs at the first moment of tomorrow, regardless of intention.
This affects the possibility of reconciliation and the exact timing of the divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ruling on giving divorce by saying 'anti taliq fi ghadin'. So when he specified the end of the day, the intended specification is more worthy of consideration than the necessary one. Its analogy: when he says 'by Allah, I will fast my entire life', and the first analogy: 'by Allah, I will fast in my life'. And similarly for 'al-dahr' and 'fi al-dahr'. Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'anti taliq fi ghadin' (you are divorced in tomorrow). The key issue is whether the man's intention to specify the end of the day is accepted in court. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that it is accepted because the word 'fi' (in) indicates a container, not coverage of the entire day. Without an intention, the divorce occurs at the first part of the day by default. But if the man explicitly intends the end of the day, his intended specification ('ta'yeen qasdi') takes precedence over the default ('ta'yeen daruri'). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they argue that 'fi ghadin' is equivalent to 'ghadan' (tomorrow), which covers the entire day. Therefore, the intention to specify a part is not accepted in court because it contradicts the apparent meaning. They draw an analogy with an oath: 'I will fast my entire life' versus 'I will fast in my life'; the former implies full coverage, the latter does not. The text supports the Sahibayn's view that 'fi' does not necessitate coverage, but Abu Hanifa's ruling is that the intention is accepted. The passage ends abruptly, but the ongoing debate is clear.

Key Terms
ظرف (zarf): A container or adverbial of time; 'fi' indicates that the action is within the time, not covering it entirely.
استیعاب (isti'ab): Full coverage or encompassment of the entire time period.
تعین ضروری (ta'yeen daruri): Necessary specification (first part of the time) when no intention is stated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The divorce is invalid
D) The wife's consent is required

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'fi' does not imply full coverage, so intention for a specific part of 'ghadin' is accepted in court according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn treat 'fi ghadin' like 'ghadan' and do not accept the intention.

When Both 'Today' and 'Tomorrow' Are Mentioned in Divorce
Ruling on which time prevails when both today and tomorrow are mentioned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the timing of divorce when a husband mentions two different times and explain why the second word is disregarded.

Core Idea
In a divorce pronouncement with two times, the first word spoken determines the timing; the second is redundant (laghw).

This lesson examines a specific issue in talaq: when a husband says both 'today' and 'tomorrow' in one pronouncement. The Hanafi rule gives priority to the first word. The reasoning is based on the linguistic nature of immediate (munajjaz) versus annexed (mudaaf) divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronouncement
Read the husband's statement and locate the two time indicators (e.g., 'today' and 'tomorrow').
2
Determine the first word
Identify which time word was spoken first in the sequence.
The order is determined by the actual utterance, not grammatical structure.
3
Apply the rule
The first word governs the timing. If it is 'today', divorce is immediate; if 'tomorrow', it is annexed to tomorrow.
The second word becomes لغو (laghw) and does not affect the ruling.
4
Understand the reasoning
Recognize that an immediate divorce (منجز) cannot be made subject to a future time, and an annexed divorce (مضاف) cannot be immediate without nullifying the annexation.
This is a linguistic principle of Arabic.
5
Confirm the outcome
State when the divorce takes effect and note which word is redundant.
The divorce is valid and occurs at the time indicated by the first word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پہلے تکلم کیا ہے۔ چنانچہ پہلی (صورت) میں یوم میں واقع ہوگی۔ اور دوسری (صورت) میں غد میں واقع ہو گی ۔ کیونکہ جب کہا الیوم تو ( طلاق فی الحال ہو گئی ۔ اور نجز اضافت ( الی الغد ) کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ہے۔ اور اگر کہا غدا تو ہوگی اضافت اور مضاف منجر نہیں ہوں ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں اضافت کو باطل کرنا ہے۔ پس دونوں صورتوں میں ( لفظ ) ثانی لغو ہو گیا۔
تشریح مسئلہ، اگر مرد نے اپنی منکوحہ سے کہا انت طالق اليوم غدایا انت طالق غدا الیوم تو دونوں وقتوں میں سے اس کو لیا جائے گا جس کا تکلم اولا کیا ہے۔ چنانچہ پہلی صورت انت طالق اليوم غدا میں طلاق غد ( کل آئندہ) میں واقع ہوگی ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب اليوم کہا تو یہ طلاق منجز ہوگئی (فی الحال ) اور منجز اضافت الى الغد کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی۔ لہذا اس صورت میں لفظ غد لغو ہو گا۔ اور اگر پہلے خدا کہا تو طلاق مضاف الى الغد ہو گئی۔ اور مضاف الى الغد منجز نہیں ہو سکتی۔ کیونکہ مضاف الى الغد کو مجز مانے میں اضافت کا باطل کرنالازم آتا ہے۔ جو درست نہیں ۔ لہذا اس صورت میں لفظ الیوم لغو ہوگا۔
He spoke first. So in the first case, divorce occurs in today; in the second case, it will occur in tomorrow. Because when he said 'today', the divorce became immediate, and it does not admit the possibility of annexation to 'tomorrow'. And if he said 'tomorrow', then it becomes annexed and the appended is not [immediate] because that would require invalidating the annexation. So in both cases, the second word becomes لغو (redundant). Explanation of the issue: If a man says to his wife 'you are divorced today tomorrow' or 'you are divorced tomorrow today', then whichever he spoke first is taken. So in the first case 'you are divorced today tomorrow', divorce occurs in tomorrow. The proof: when he said 'today', the divorce became immediate, and immediate does not admit annexation to tomorrow. Hence tomorrow is لغو. And if he said 'tomorrow' first, the divorce is annexed to tomorrow, and annexed to tomorrow cannot be immediate because treating an annexed to tomorrow as immediate would nullify the annexation, which is not valid. So in this case 'today' is لغو.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a divorce pronouncement that mentions two different times, such as 'today' and 'tomorrow'. The key principle is that the first word spoken determines the timing of the divorce. If the husband says 'today' first, the divorce takes effect immediately, and the mention of 'tomorrow' is لغو (redundant and disregarded). If he says 'tomorrow' first, the divorce is delayed until tomorrow, and 'today' is redundant. The reasoning is based on the nature of the words: 'today' (اليوم) when used in a divorce creates an immediate (منجز) divorce, which cannot be annexed to a future time. 'Tomorrow' (غدا) when used first creates an annexed (مضاف) divorce, which cannot be made immediate without nullifying the annexation. This is a linguistic principle of the Arabic language. The author (the commentator) is explaining the ruling from the Hidayah.

Key Terms
طلق (talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
منجز (munajjaz): Immediate; a divorce that takes effect instantly without being tied to a future condition or time.
مضاف (mudaaf): Annexed; a divorce tied to a future time, such as 'you are divorced tomorrow'.
لغو (laghw): Redundant; a word that is ineffective and disregarded in the legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Tomorrow
C) Both today and tomorrow
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
In a divorce pronouncement mentioning two different times, the first word spoken governs; the second is redundant.

Divorce on 'Tomorrow' with Intention: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the acceptance of intention in divorce pronounced with 'fi ghade', explain the linguistic reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
'Fi' does not require full coverage; intentional determination outweighs necessity.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of the preposition 'fi' (in/on) when a husband says 'you are divorced on tomorrow' and later claims he intended the end of the day. The disagreement hinges on whether 'fi' implies that the action covers the entire day (isti'ab) or merely occurs within it. Understanding this linguistic nuance is essential for correctly applying the rules of divorce and oath-taking.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
State clearly what Abu Hanifa holds: the husband's intention of the end of the day is accepted in court, and divorce occurs at that time.
This is the position of the Imam of the school.
2
Identify the opponent's view (Sahibayn)
State the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the intention is not accepted in court (only diyana), and divorce occurs at the beginning of tomorrow.
They are the two chief students.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument that 'fi ghade' is equivalent to 'ghade' (without fi), which requires comprehension (isti'ab) of all of tomorrow.
They rely on the annexation to 'all of tomorrow'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: 'fi' indicates a container (zarf) and does not require the contained act to fill it; the determination of the first part is only due to necessity, but intentional determination is stronger.
The Hidayah supports this by contrasting with 'ghade'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Identify the practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, the husband is believed and the divorce occurs at the end; according to Sahibayn, it occurs immediately. Then state that the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.
The Hidayah explicitly favors Abu Hanifa.

انت طالق في غد سے طلاق دینے کا حکم
ولو قال انت طالق فى غدوقال نويت اخر النهار دين فى القضاء عندابي حنيفة وقالا لايدين في القضاء خاصة لانه وصفها بالطلاق فى جميع الغد فصار بمنزلة قوله غدا على مابينا ولهذا يقع في اول جزء منه عند عدم النية وهذا لان حذف فى واثباته سواء لانه ظرف فى الحالين ولابي حنيفة انه نوى حقيقة كلامه لان كلمة في للظرف والظرفية لاتقتضى الاستيعاب وتعين الجزء الاول ضرورة عدم المزاحم فاذاعين آخر النهار كان التعين القصدى اولى بالا عتبار من الضرورى بخلاف قوله غدا لانه يقتضى الاستيعاب حيث وصفها بهذه لهفة مضافا الى جميع الغد نظيره اذاقال والله لاصو من عمرى ونظير الاول والله لاصو من في عمري وعلى هذا الدهرو في الدهر
ترجمہ اور اگر کہا انت طالق فی غدہ اور کہا اس نے کہ میں نے آخر نہار کی نیت کی ہے۔ تو قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اور صاحبین نے فرمایا نہیں تصدیق کی جائے گی قضاء خاص طور پر ۔ کیونکہ اس نے عورت کو طلاق کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے۔ جمیع غد میں ۔ پس ہو گیا بمنزلہ اسکے قول غدا کے اسی دلیل پر جو ہم نے بیان کی ہے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے غدر کے اول جزء میں طلاق واقع ہوگی عدم نیست کے وقت ۔ اور یہ اسلئے ہے کہ فی کا حذف کرنا اور اس کو باقی رکھنا دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیونکہ غد دونوں صورتوں میں ظرف ہے۔ اور ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس نے اپنے کلام کی حقیقت کی نیت کی ہے۔ کیونکہ کلمہ فی ظرف کیلئے ہے۔ اور ظرفیت استیعاب کا تقاضا نہیں کرتی ہے۔ اور جزء اول کا متعین ہونا عدم مزاحم کی وجہ سے ہے۔ پس جب متعین کر دیا آخر نہار کو تو تعین قصدی اولی ہے اعتبار ضروری کے مقابلہ میں بخلاف اس کے قول غدا کے کیونکہ لفظ غدا استیعاب کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ عورت کو اس صفت کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے جمیع غد کی طرف منسوب کرتے ہوئے ۔ اسکی نظیر جبکہ کہا غدا میں اپنی زندگی بھر روزہ رکھوں گا۔ اور اول کی نظیر بخدا میں اپنی زندگی میں روزہ رکھوں گا۔ اور اسی بنیاد پر الدھر اور فی الدھر ہے۔
تشریح مسئلہ، اگر کسی نے انت طالق فی غر کہا اور آخر نہار کی نیت کی، تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی ۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک صرف قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی ۔ البتہ دیا نہ تصدیق کر لی جائے گی۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے عورت کو جمیع مد میں وصف طلاق کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے لہذا فی غد ، بمنزلۃ غد کے ہو گیا۔ یعنی اس نے عموم میں تخصیص کی نیت کی ہے۔ اور تخصیص عموم خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ اس وجہ سے قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائیگی ۔ البتہ دیا نئے تصدیق کر لی جائیگی۔ اور چونکہ فی غد- غذا کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے ہے اگر کسی جز کی نیت نہیں کی ہے تو غد کے اول جز میں طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اور یہ (عدم نیت کے وقت غدر کے اول جز میں طلاق کا واقع ہونا ) اسلئے ہے کہ فی کا ذکر کرنا اور حذف کرنا دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیوں کہ دونوں صورتوں میں کلمہ فی ظرف ہے۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے آخر نہار کی نیت اپنے کلام کی حقیقت کی نیت کی ہے۔ کیونکہ کلمہ فی ظرف کیلئے ہے۔ اور ظرفیت استیعاب مظروف کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ مظروف کبھی ظرف کے اجزاء میں سے ایک جز میں پایا جاتا ہے۔ اور کبھی ظرف مظروف سے بھر جاتا ہے۔ اور مظروف کا استیعاب بھی کر لیتا ہے۔ پس اس کا کلام دونوں صورتوں میں احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ لہذا . جب اس شخص نے ایک احتمال کی نیت کی تو گویا اپنے کلام کے حقیقی معنی کی نیت کی ۔ اس وجہ سے امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی ۔
اور صاحبین کا یہ کہنا کہ عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول متعین ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول کا متعین ہونا عدم مزاحم کی وجہ سے ہے۔ مگر جب آخر نہار کو تعین کر دیا تو آخر نہر کی تعیین قصدی ہوئی۔ اور عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول کی تعیین کا ضرورۃ اعتبار کیا گیا ہے۔ اور ظاہر ہے کہ جزء اول کا ضرورۃ اعتبار کرنے کے مقابلہ میں تعیین قصدی اولی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ بخلاف غدا سے فرماتے ہیں کہ فی غر کو خدا پر قیاس کرنا صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ غدا استیعاب کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ یعنی عورت کو صفت طلاق کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے جمیع غد کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے ۔ عذر بلا ذكر في كي نظير والله لاصو من عمری اور بذکرفی کی نظیر والله لا صومن فی عمری ۔ پہلی صورت میں پوری زندگی روزے رکھے گا۔ علاوہ ایام منہی عنہا کے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں پوری زندگی میں تھوڑی دیر کا روزہ بھی کافی ہوگا۔ ایسے ہی الدھر اور فی الدھر میں فرق ہے۔ یعنی والله لاصو من الدھر بغیر فی کے اور والله لا صو من فی الدھر ۔ پہلی صورت میں اپنی یمین سے اس وقت بری ہو گا جب پورے زمانے کے روزے رکھے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں تھوڑی دیر کے روزے سے بھی بری ہو جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم
Ruling on divorcing by saying 'you are divorced on tomorrow'. And if he said 'you are divorced on tomorrow' and he said 'I intended the end of the day', it will be believed in court according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he will not be believed specifically in court, because he described her with divorce in all of tomorrow, so it becomes like his saying 'tomorrow' according to what we explained. And for this reason it occurs at the first part of it when there is no intention. And this because removing 'fi' and affirming it are the same, because it is a time adverb in both cases. And for Abu Hanifa: he intended the reality of his speech, because the word 'fi' is for adverbiality, and adverbiality does not require comprehension. And the determination of the first part is due to necessity of no competitor. So when he specifically intended the end of the day, the intended determination takes precedence over the necessary one. Unlike his saying 'tomorrow' because it requires comprehension, since he described her with this attribute annexed to all of tomorrow. Its analogy: when he says 'by Allah, I will fast my entire life', and the first analogy: 'by Allah, I will fast in my life'. And on this principle is الدھر and فی الدھر. Translation: And if he said 'you are divorced on tomorrow' and he said that he intended the end of the day, then in court it will be believed according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and the two companions said: he will not be believed in court specifically. Because he described the woman with divorce in all of tomorrow, so it becomes like his saying 'tomorrow' according to the proof we mentioned. And for this reason, divorce occurs in the first part of tomorrow when there is no intention. And this is because removing 'fi' and keeping it are equal, because tomorrow is a time adverb in both cases. And the proof of Abu Hanifa is that he intended the reality of his speech, because the word 'fi' is for time adverb, and adverbiality does not require comprehension. And the determination of the first part is due to the absence of a competitor. So when he specifically intended the end of the day, the intended determination is more worthy of consideration than the necessary one. Unlike his saying 'tomorrow' because the word 'tomorrow' requires comprehension, since he described her with this attribute by annexing to all of tomorrow. Its analogy: when he said 'in his life I will fast' and the first analogy: 'by Allah, I will fast in my life'. And on this basis is الدهر and في الدهر. Explanation: If someone said 'you are divorced on tomorrow' and intended the end of the day, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa it will be believed in court. And according to the Sahibayn, it will not be believed in court, but it will be believed diyana (in conscience). The proof of the Sahibayn is that the person described the woman with the attribute of divorce in all of tomorrow, so 'fi ghade' becomes like 'ghade'. That is, he intended a specification of the general, and specification of the general is contrary to the apparent. For this reason it will not be believed in court, but it will be believed diyana. And since 'fi ghade' is in the rank of 'ghade', therefore if he did not intend any part, divorce occurs in the first part of tomorrow. And this (occurrence in the first part when there is no intention) is because mentioning 'fi' and omitting it are equal, since in both cases the word 'fi' is a time adverb. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the person intended the end of the day; he intended the reality of his speech. Because the word 'fi' is for time adverb, and adverbiality does not require comprehension of the thing contained. Rather, the thing contained is sometimes found in one part of the container, and sometimes the container is filled by the contained, and it also comprehends the contained. So his speech admits both possibilities. Therefore, when the person intended one possibility, he essentially intended the real meaning of his speech. For this reason, Imam sahib says that it will be believed in court. And as for the Sahibayn's saying that in case of no intention the first part is determined, the answer is that in case of no intention the determination of the first part is due to the absence of a competitor. But when he specifically intended the end of the day, the determination of the end of the day is intentional, and in case of no intention the determination of the first part was given consideration necessarily. And it is obvious that intentional determination is superior to necessary determination. The author of Hidayah says, 'unlike tomorrow', that analogizing 'fi ghade' to 'ghade' is not correct. Because 'ghade' requires comprehension, i.e., he described the woman with the attribute of divorce while relating to all of tomorrow. The analogy without 'fi' is 'by Allah, I will fast my whole life', and with 'fi' is 'by Allah, I will fast in my life'. In the first case, he will fast the whole life, except for forbidden days. In the second case, fasting for a short while in the whole life is sufficient. Similarly, there is a difference between الدهر and في الدهر. That is, 'by Allah, I will fast for all time' without 'fi', and 'by Allah, I will fast in all time'. In the first case, he will be free from his oath only when he fasts the entire time. In the second case, he will be free with a short fast. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two chief students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue is whether a husband who says 'you are divorced on tomorrow' (في غد) and intends specifically the end of the day is believed in court regarding that intention. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that he is believed, because the word 'fi' (in/on) does not require the action to cover the entire day; it allows the action to occur in any part. The Sahibayn argue that 'fi ghade' is equivalent to just 'ghade' (tomorrow) which implies the entire day, so specifying a part is against the apparent meaning; therefore, he is not believed in court but may be believed diyana (in his conscience). The author of Al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) supports Abu Hanifa's view, emphasizing that 'ghade' without 'fi' requires comprehension (isti'ab), but 'fi ghade' does not. The analogy with oaths on fasting illustrates the linguistic principle: saying 'I will fast my whole life' (without 'fi') requires fasting every day, whereas saying 'I will fast in my life' (with 'fi') allows fasting for even a short period. This confirms the distinction between words that require comprehension and those that do not. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the Hidayah.

Key Terms
في (fi): A preposition meaning 'in' or 'on'; when used with time, it indicates that an action occurs within that time without necessitating its entire duration.
ظرف (zarf): A time or place adverb; in this context, it refers to 'ghad' (tomorrow) serving as the container for the action of divorce.
استيعاب (isti'ab): Comprehension; the requirement that an action covers the entirety of the time or space mentioned.
تعين قصدى (ta'ayyun qasdi): Intentional determination; when the speaker specifically intends a particular part of the time.
تعين ضرورى (ta'ayyun daruri): Necessary determination; when a part is determined by necessity (e.g., the first part when no specific intention is made).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the husband's intention of the end of the day accepted when he says 'you are divorced on tomorrow'?
A) Because 'fi' requires comprehension
B) Because 'fi' does not require comprehension, so the intention of a part is valid
C) Because the husband must be believed in all cases
D) Because divorce cannot occur at night

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the preposition 'fi' in a divorce pronouncement removes the requirement of full-day coverage, so the husband's specific intention of a part is accepted in court.

Talaq — Divorce

8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Talaq with 'fī ghadin' and the Role of Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on talaq effectiveness with 'fī ghadin' phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the talaq phrase 'fī ghadin', explain the linguistic basis for each position, and apply the distinction to analogous oath expressions.

Core Idea
The particle 'fī' allows partial containment, so intent can specify a part of tomorrow in talaq.

When a husband says 'you are divorced tomorrow' using the phrase 'fī ghadin', a subtle linguistic question arises: does this bind him to the entire day, or can he intend a specific part? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The dispute turns on the function of the Arabic particle 'fī' as a container (ẓarf) and whether it demands full encompassing. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to discern how linguistic analysis shapes fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly what each side holds regarding the validity and timing of talaq when the phrase 'fī ghadin' is used with a specific intention (e.g., last part of the day).
Imam Abu Hanifa: talaq occurs at the intended part and is enforceable in court. Sahibayn: talaq occurs at the first part and the intention is only inwardly accepted.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Examine why they equate 'fī ghadin' with 'ghadan' and why they consider specification a departure from the apparent meaning.
Their key claim: mentioning 'fī' or omitting it is equivalent because both function as a ẓarf.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's proof
Understand his linguistic argument that 'fī' indicates containment, which does not require full encompassment; therefore his speech is probable, and intending one probability is intending the true meaning.
The key point: containment can be partial, so the time can be restricted by intention.
4
Examine the tarjih and response
See how the author of Al-Hidayah refutes the analogy to 'ghadan' and why intentional determination is superior to necessary determination.
The author adopts Imam Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi.
5
Apply the principle to oath analogies
Work through the examples from oaths ('umri vs. fī 'umri, 'dahr vs. fī dahr) to reinforce the linguistic rule that 'fī' relaxes the requirement of full duration.
This shows that the same particle behaves consistently across different contexts.

صاحبین کے نزدیک صرف قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی ۔ البتہ دیا نہ تصدیق کر لی جائے گی۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے عورت کو جمیع مد میں وصف طلاق کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے لہذا فی غد ، بمنزلۃ غد کے ہو گیا۔ یعنی اس نے عموم میں تخصیص کی نیت کی ہے۔ اور تخصیص عموم خلاف ظاہر ہے۔ اس وجہ سے قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائیگی ۔ البتہ دیا نئے تصدیق کر لی جائیگی۔ اور چونکہ فی غد- غذا کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے ہے اگر کسی جز کی نیت نہیں کی ہے تو غد کے اول جز میں طلاق واقع ہوگی۔ اور یہ (عدم نیت کے وقت غدر کے اول جز میں طلاق کا واقع ہونا ) اسلئے ہے کہ فی کا ذکر کرنا اور حذف کرنا دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیوں کہ دونوں صورتوں میں کلمہ فی ظرف ہے۔
امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے آخر نہار کی نیت اپنے کلام کی حقیقت کی نیت کی ہے۔ کیونکہ کلمہ فی ظرف کیلئے ہے۔ اور ظرفیت استیعاب مظروف کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ مظروف کبھی ظرف کے اجزاء میں سے ایک جز میں پایا جاتا ہے۔ اور کبھی ظرف مظروف سے بھر جاتا ہے۔ اور مظروف کا استیعاب بھی کر لیتا ہے۔ پس اس کا کلام دونوں صورتوں میں احتمال رکھتا ہے۔ لہذا . جب اس شخص نے ایک احتمال کی نیت کی تو گویا اپنے کلام کے حقیقی معنی کی نیت کی ۔ اس وجہ سے امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ قضاء تصدیق کی جائے گی ۔
اور صاحبین کا یہ کہنا کہ عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول متعین ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول کا متعین ہونا عدم مزاحم کی وجہ سے ہے۔ مگر جب آخر نہار کو تعین کر دیا تو آخر نہر کی تعیین قصدی ہوئی۔ اور عدم نیت کی صورت میں جزء اول کی تعیین کا ضرورۃ اعتبار کیا گیا ہے۔ اور ظاہر ہے کہ جزء اول کا ضرورۃ اعتبار کرنے کے مقابلہ میں تعیین قصدی اولی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ بخلاف غدا سے فرماتے ہیں کہ فی غر کو خدا پر قیاس کرنا صحیح نہیں۔ کیونکہ غدا استیعاب کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ یعنی عورت کو صفت طلاق کے ساتھ متصف کیا ہے جمیع غد کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے ۔
عذر بلا ذكر في كي نظير والله لاصو من عمری اور بذکرفی کی نظیر والله لا صومن فی عمری ۔ پہلی صورت میں پوری زندگی روزے رکھے گا۔ علاوہ ایام منہی عنہا کے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں پوری زندگی میں تھوڑی دیر کا روزہ بھی کافی ہوگا۔ ایسے ہی الدھر اور فی الدھر میں فرق ہے۔ یعنی والله لاصو من الدھر بغیر فی کے اور والله لا صو من فی الدھر ۔ پہلی صورت میں اپنی یمین سے اس وقت بری ہو گا جب پورے زمانے کے روزے رکھے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں تھوڑی دیر کے روزے سے بھی بری ہو جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم
According to the Sahibayn, in court (qaḍā’) it will not be accepted, but inwardly (diyānatan) it will be accepted. Their proof is that this person has described the woman with the attribute of divorce in the entire time [of tomorrow], therefore 'fī ghadin' becomes like 'ghadan' (tomorrow). That is, he intended specification (takhṣīṣ) within generality, and specification within generality is contrary to the apparent meaning. For this reason, it will not be accepted in court, but inwardly it will be accepted. And since 'fī ghadin' is in the status of 'ghadan', if he did not intend any part, divorce occurs in the first part of tomorrow. The reason is that mentioning 'fī' and omitting it are equivalent, because in both cases the word 'fī' is a particle of container (ẓarf).
Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that this person intended the end of the day, which is the reality of his speech. Because the particle 'fī' is for containment, and containment does not require encompassing the entire contained object; rather, the contained is sometimes found in one part of the container, and sometimes the container is filled and encompasses the contained. So his speech is probable in both ways. Therefore, when he intended one probability, it is as if he intended the true meaning of his speech. For this reason, Imam Abu Hanifa says that it will be accepted in court.
As for the Sahibayn's claim that in the absence of intention, the first part is determined, the response is that in the absence of intention, the first part's determination is due to the absence of any hindrance. But when he determines the end of the day, that determination is intentional. In the case of no intention, the determination of the first part is considered necessary. It is obvious that intentional determination is superior to necessary consideration. The author of Al-Hidayah says that analogizing 'fī ghadin' to 'ghadan' is not correct because 'ghadan' requires encompassing, meaning he described the woman with the attribute of divorce relating to all of tomorrow.
The example without mentioning 'fī' is like 'By Allah, I will certainly fast for an entire lifetime' (والله لأصومن عمری) and with mentioning 'fī' is like 'By Allah, I will certainly fast in a lifetime' (والله لأصومن في عمري). In the first case, he will fast his entire life except prohibited days. In the second case, even a brief fast during his lifetime suffices. Similarly, there is a difference between 'ad-dahr' and 'fī ad-dahr': 'By Allah, I will fast for all time' without 'fī' versus 'By Allah, I will fast in time' with 'fī'. In the first, he is released from his oath only when he fasts the entire period; in the second, even a short fast releases him. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the effectiveness of a divorce statement that includes the phrase 'fī ghadin' (في غدٍ – 'on tomorrow' or 'in tomorrow'). The core issue is whether the particle 'fī' restricts the talaq to a part of tomorrow or covers the entire day. The Sahibayn argue that 'fī ghadin' is legally equivalent to 'ghadan' (simply 'tomorrow'), so the apparent meaning is the entire day; therefore, if the husband intends a specific part, it is a deviation from the apparent meaning and only accepted inwardly, not in court. Without any intention, divorce falls in the first part of tomorrow because mentioning 'fī' or omitting it is the same. Imam Abu Hanifa contends that 'fī' does not demand full encompassment; it only indicates containment, which can be partial. Thus, when the husband intends the last part of the day, he is intending the true linguistic meaning, and his intention is accepted both inwardly and in court. The author of Al-Hidayah supports Imam Abu Hanifa. To illustrate, the passage draws an analogy from oaths: saying 'Wallah la asumann 'umri' (for whole life) vs. 'Wallah la asumann fi 'umri' (in my life) – the latter allows a short fast. This shows that 'fī' does not entail full duration. The same applies to 'ad-dahr' vs. 'fī ad-dahr'. Thus, the analogy supports Imam Abu Hanifa's view that 'fī ghadin' can be restricted by intention.

Key Terms
فی غد (fī ghadin): The phrase 'on tomorrow' or 'in tomorrow'; used to specify the time of divorce.
ظرف (ẓarf): A particle of time or place indicating containment; in this context, 'fī' is a ẓarf.
تصدیق قضاء (taṣdīq qaḍā’): Acceptance or validation by a court of law; opposite of diyānatan (inwardly).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a man says 'anti taliq fī ghadin' and intends the last part of the day, what is the ruling?
A) Talaq does not occur because the intention contradicts the apparent meaning.
B) Talaq occurs at the first part of tomorrow, and the intention is accepted only inwardly.
C) Talaq occurs at the end of the day, and the intention is accepted in court.
D) Talaq occurs immediately at the time of utterance.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fī' allows partial containment, so intention can specify a part of a time period in talaq and in oaths; the mufta bihi is Imam Abu Hanifa's view that such intention is accepted both inwardly and in court.
